<table cellspacing="0" cellpadding="0" style="background:transparent; width:100%;">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="width:50%; text-align:left;"><b><a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the United States House of Representatives">Speaker</a> before election</b><br />
<p><a href="/wiki/Tom_Foley" title="Tom Foley">Tom Foley</a><br />
<a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(US)" class="mw-redirect" title="Democratic Party (US)">Democratic</a>
</p>
</td>
<td style="width:50%; text-align:right;"><b>Elected <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the United States House of Representatives">Speaker</a></b> <br />
<p><a href="/wiki/Newt_Gingrich" title="Newt Gingrich">Newt Gingrich</a><br />
<a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(US)" class="mw-redirect" title="Republican Party (US)">Republican</a>
</p>
</td></tr></tbody></table></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>1994 United States House of Representatives elections</b> (also known as the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Revolution" title="Republican Revolution">Republican Revolution</a>) were held on November 8, 1994, in the middle of <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a>'s first term. As a result of a 54-seat swing in membership from the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a> to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Republican_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Republican Party">Republican Party</a>, Republicans gained a majority of seats in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">United States House of Representatives</a> for the first time since <a href="/wiki/1952_United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections" title="1952 United States House of Representatives elections">1952</a>. It was also the largest seat gain for the party since <a href="/wiki/1946_United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections" title="1946 United States House of Representatives elections">1946</a>, and the largest for either party since <a href="/wiki/1948_United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections" title="1948 United States House of Representatives elections">1948</a>, and characterized a <a href="/wiki/Political_realignment" title="Political realignment">political realignment</a> in American politics. 
</p><p>Democrats had run the House since <a href="/wiki/84th_United_States_Congress" title="84th United States Congress">1955</a>, and for all but four years (1947–49 and 1953–55) since <a href="/wiki/72nd_United_States_Congress" title="72nd United States Congress">1931</a>. But in 1994 the Republican Party ran against President Clinton's <a href="/wiki/Clinton_health_care_plan_of_1993" title="Clinton health care plan of 1993">proposed healthcare reform</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-google_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> benefited from impressions of corruption created by the <a href="/wiki/Whitewater_investigation" class="mw-redirect" title="Whitewater investigation">Whitewater investigation</a>, and picked up a majority of voters who had voted for <a href="/wiki/Ross_Perot" title="Ross Perot">Ross Perot</a> in 1992. The Republicans argued that Clinton had abandoned the centrist <a href="/wiki/New_Democrat" class="mw-redirect" title="New Democrat">New Democrat</a> platform he campaigned on during the 1992 Presidential election and reverted to big government solutions. The GOP ran on Newt Gingrich's <a href="/wiki/Contract_with_America" title="Contract with America">Contract with America</a>.
</p><p>The incumbent <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the United States House of Representatives">Speaker of the House</a>, Democrat <a href="/wiki/Tom_Foley" title="Tom Foley">Tom Foley</a>, lost reelection in his district, becoming the first sitting Speaker to do so since <a href="/wiki/Galusha_A._Grow" title="Galusha A. Grow">Galusha Grow</a> in <a href="/wiki/1862_and_1863_United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections" title="1862 and 1863 United States House of Representatives elections">1863</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Other major upsets included the defeat of powerful long-serving Representatives such as Ways and Means Chairman <a href="/wiki/Dan_Rostenkowski" title="Dan Rostenkowski">Dan Rostenkowski</a> and Judiciary Chairman <a href="/wiki/Jack_Brooks_(American_politician)" title="Jack Brooks (American politician)">Jack Brooks</a>. In all, 34 incumbents, all Democrats, were defeated. Republicans also won a number of seats held by retiring Democrats. No Republican incumbents lost reelection, but Democrats won four open Republican-held seats.
</p><p>The incumbent Republican <a href="/wiki/Minority_Whip_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" class="mw-redirect" title="Minority Whip of the United States House of Representatives">Minority whip</a>, <a href="/wiki/Newt_Gingrich" title="Newt Gingrich">Newt Gingrich</a>, was re-elected in the Republican landslide and became Speaker as the incumbent Republican <a href="/wiki/Minority_Leader_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" class="mw-redirect" title="Minority Leader of the United States House of Representatives">Minority Leader</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_H._Michel" title="Robert H. Michel">Robert H. Michel</a>, retired. The incumbent Democratic <a href="/wiki/Majority_Leader_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" class="mw-redirect" title="Majority Leader of the United States House of Representatives">Majority Leader</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dick_Gephardt" title="Dick Gephardt">Dick Gephardt</a>, became Minority Leader. The new House leadership, under the Republicans, promised to bring a dozen legislative proposals to a vote in the first 100 days of the session, although the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senate</a> did not always follow suit. A significant <a href="/wiki/Political_realignment" title="Political realignment">political realignment</a>, the South underwent a drastic transformation. Before the election, House Democrats outnumbered House Republicans in the South. Afterwards, with the Republicans having picked up a total of 19 Southern seats, they were able to outnumber Democrats in the South for the first time since <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Era" class="mw-redirect" title="Reconstruction Era">Reconstruction</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The Republicans would go on to remain the majority party of the House for the following 12 years, until the <a href="/wiki/2006_United_States_House_elections" class="mw-redirect" title="2006 United States House elections">2006 elections</a>.
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_right">Religious right</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1994_United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Religious right">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Evangelicals were an important group within the electorate and a significant voting block in the Republican party. The national exit poll by Mitofsky International showed 27% of all voters identified themselves as a born-again or evangelical Christians, up from 18% in 1988 and 24% in 1992. Republican House candidates outpolled Democrats among white evangelicals by a massive 52 points, 76% to 24%.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to a survey sponsored by the Christian Coalition, 33 percent of the 1994 voters were "religious conservatives," up from 24 percent in 1992 and 18 percent in 1988 (<i>CQ Weekly Report</i>), November 19, 1994, p.&#160;3364; in the 1994 exit poll, 38 percent identified themselves as "conservatives," compared with 30 percent in 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Incumbents_defeated">Incumbents defeated</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1994_United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Incumbents defeated">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Every Republican incumbent standing won re-election.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Democrats">Democrats</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1994_United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Democrats">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Thirty-four incumbent Democrats (including 16 "freshmen") were defeated in 1994. Democrats from <a href="#Washington">Washington</a> lost the most seats (5).
</p>
<ul><li><span data-sort-value="Arizona06&#160;!"><a href="/wiki/Arizona%27s_6th_congressional_district" title="Arizona&#39;s 6th congressional district">Arizona&#160;6</a></span>: <a href="/wiki/Karan_English" title="Karan English">Karan English</a></li>
<li><span data-sort-value="California01&#160;!"><a href="/wiki/California%27s_1st_congressional_district" title="California&#39;s 1st congressional district">California&#160;1</a></span>: <a href="/wiki/Dan_Hamburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Dan Hamburg">Dan Hamburg</a></li>
<li><span data-sort-value="California19&#160;!"><a href="/wiki/California%27s_19th_congressional_district" title="California&#39;s 19th congressional district">California&#160;19</a></span>: <a href="/wiki/Richard_H._Lehman" title="Richard H. Lehman">Richard H. Lehman</a></li>
<li><span data-sort-value="California49&#160;!"><a href="/wiki/California%27s_49th_congressional_district" title="California&#39;s 49th congressional district">California&#160;49</a></span>: <a href="/wiki/Lynn_Schenk" title="Lynn Schenk">Lynn Schenk</a></li>
<li><span data-sort-value="Georgia07&#160;!"><a href="/wiki/Georgia%27s_7th_congressional_district" title="Georgia&#39;s 7th congressional district">Georgia&#160;7</a></span>: <a href="/wiki/George_Darden" title="George Darden">George Darden</a></li>
<li><span data-sort-value="Georgia10&#160;!"><a href="/wiki/Georgia%27s_10th_congressional_district" title="Georgia&#39;s 10th congressional district">Georgia&#160;10</a></span>: <a href="/wiki/Don_Johnson_Jr." title="Don Johnson Jr.">Don Johnson Jr.</a></li>
<li><span data-sort-value="Idaho01&#160;!"><a href="/wiki/Idaho%27s_1st_congressional_district" title="Idaho&#39;s 1st congressional district">Idaho&#160;1</a></span>: <a href="/wiki/Larry_LaRocco" title="Larry LaRocco">Larry LaRocco</a></li>
<li><span data-sort-value="Illinois05&#160;!"><a href="/wiki/Illinois%27s_5th_congressional_district" title="Illinois&#39;s 5th congressional district">Illinois&#160;5</a></span>: <a href="/wiki/Dan_Rostenkowski" title="Dan Rostenkowski">Dan Rostenkowski</a></li>
